style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Scotland in the Late Middle Ages</b>, between the deaths of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a> in 1286 and <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> in 1513, established its independence from England under figures including <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> in the late 13th century and <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert Bruce</a> in the 14th century. In the 15th century under the <a href="/wiki/Stewart_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Stewart Dynasty">Stewart Dynasty</a>, despite a turbulent political history, the Crown gained greater political control at the expense of independent lords and regained most of its lost territory to approximately the modern borders of the country. However, the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a> with France led to the heavy defeat of a Scottish army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Battle of Flodden</a> in 1513 and the death of the king <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a>, which would be followed by a long minority and a period of political instability.\n</p><p>The economy of Scotland developed slowly in this period and a population of perhaps a little under a million by the middle of the 14th century began to decline after the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, falling to perhaps half a million by the beginning of the 16th century. Different social systems and cultures developed in the lowland and highland regions of the country as <a href="/wiki/Scots_Gaelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots Gaelic">Gaelic</a> remained the most common language north of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tay" title="River Tay">Tay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a> dominated in the south, where it became the language of the ruling elite, government and a new national literature. There were significant changes in religion which saw <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicant</a> friars and new devotions expand, particularly in the developing <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a>.\n</p><p>By the end of the period Scotland had adopted many of the major tenets of the <a href="/wiki/European_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="European Renaissance">European Renaissance</a> in art, architecture and literature and produced a developed educational system. This period has been seen one in which a clear national identity emerged in Scotland, as well as significant distinctions between different regions of the country which would be particularly significant in the period of the Reformation.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div id="Wars_of_Independence_1286.E2.80.931371"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_of_Independence_1286\xe2\x80\x931371">Wars of Independence 1286\xe2\x80\x931371</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Wars of Independence 1286\xe2\x80\x931371">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="John">John</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: John">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Balliol" title="John Balliol">John Balliol</a></div>\n<p>The death of king <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a> in 1286, and the subsequent death of his granddaughter and heir <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret</a> (called "the Maid of Norway") in 1290, left 14 rivals for succession. To prevent civil war the Scottish magnates asked <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a> to arbitrate. He extracted legal recognition that the realm of Scotland was held as a feudal dependency to the throne of England before choosing <a href="/wiki/John_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="John I of Scotland">John Balliol</a>, the man with the strongest claim, who became king as John I (30 November 1292).<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Robert Bruce of Annandale, the next strongest claimant, accepted this outcome with reluctance. Over the next few years Edward I used the concessions he had gained to systematically undermine both the authority of King John and the independence of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In 1295 John, on the urgings of his chief councillors, entered into an alliance with France, the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1296 Edward invaded Scotland, deposing King John. The following year <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_de_Moray" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew de Moray">Andrew Murrey</a> raised forces to resist the occupation and under their joint leadership an English army was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>. Murrey died of wounds after the battle and for a short time Wallace ruled Scotland in the name of John Balliol as <a href="/wiki/Guardian_of_Scotland" title="Guardian of Scotland">Guardian</a> of the realm.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Edward came north in person and defeated Wallace at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Falkirk_(1298)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Falkirk (1298)">Battle of Falkirk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Wallace escaped but probably resigned as Guardian of Scotland. In 1305 he fell into the hands of the English, who executed him for treason despite the fact that he owed no allegiance to England.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_I">Robert I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Robert I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/170px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/255px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_the_Bruce.jpg/340px-Robert_the_Bruce.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="2362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_the_Bruce.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue near Stirling commemorating <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert I</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Rivals <a href="/wiki/John_III_Comyn,_Lord_of_Badenoch" title="John III Comyn, Lord of Badenoch">John Comyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert I of Scotland">Robert the Bruce</a>, grandson of the claimant Robert Bruce of Annandale, were appointed as joint guardians in Wallace\'s place.<sup id="cite_ref-Ross1999_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ross1999-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> On 10 February 1306, Bruce participated in the murder of Comyn, at Greyfriars Kirk in <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Less than seven weeks later, on 25 March Bruce was crowned as king Robert I at Scone. However, Edward\'s forces overran the country after defeating Bruce\'s small army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Methven" title="Battle of Methven">Battle of Methven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Despite the excommunication of Bruce and his followers by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Pope Clement V</a>, his support grew; and by 1314, with the help of leading nobles such as <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_Lord_of_Douglas" title="James Douglas, Lord of Douglas">Sir James Douglas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Randolph,_1st_Earl_of_Moray" title="Thomas Randolph, 1st Earl of Moray">Earl of Moray</a>, only the castles at Bothwell and Stirling remained under English control.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Edward I had died in 1307 and his heir <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> moved an army north to break the siege of <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a> and reassert control. They were defeated by forces under Robert I at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314, securing <i>de facto</i> independence.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 1320 the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath" title="Declaration of Arbroath">Declaration of Arbroath</a>, a remonstrance to the Pope from the nobles of Scotland, helped to convince <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a> to overturn the earlier excommunication and nullify the various acts of submission by Scottish kings to English ones so that Scotland\'s sovereignty could be recognised by the major European dynasties. The Declaration has also been seen as one of the most important documents in the development of a Scottish national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Robert\'s brother <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bruce" title="Edward Bruce">Edward Bruce</a> carried out a series of <a href="/wiki/Bruce_campaign_in_Ireland" title="Bruce campaign in Ireland">campaigns</a> against English forces in Ireland and was declared <a href="/wiki/High_King_of_Ireland" title="High King of Ireland">High King</a>. The campaigns in Ireland, although ultimately unsuccessful, opened the prospect of what has been characterised as "Pan-Gaelic Greater Scotia" under the Bruce dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Robert\'s forces carried out a series of raids of Northern England, defeating an English army in 1327 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stanhope_Park" title="Battle of Stanhope Park">Battle of Stanhope Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Robert\'s victories contributed to the deposition of Edward II and Robert was able to take advantage of the minority of his son <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> to secure the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh-Northampton" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Edinburgh-Northampton">Treaty of Edinburgh-Northampton</a>, signed in May 1328, which recognised Scotland as an independent kingdom, and Bruce as its king.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="David_II">David II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: David II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bruce,_king_of_Scotland,_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg/170px-David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg" width="170" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg/255px-David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg/340px-David_Bruce%2C_king_of_Scotland%2C_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg 2x" data-file-width="494" data-file-height="441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bruce,_king_of_Scotland,_acknowledges_Edward_III_as_his_feudal_lord.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a> (right) and <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> of England (left).</div></div></div>\n<p>Robert I died in 1329, leaving his five-year-old son to reign as <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a>. During his minority, the country was ruled by a series of governors, two of whom died as a result of a renewed invasion by English forces from 1332. This was on the pretext of restoring <a href="/wiki/Edward_Balliol" title="Edward Balliol">Edward Balliol</a>, son of John Balliol, to the Scottish throne, thus starting the Second War of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Despite victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dupplin_Moor" title="Battle of Dupplin Moor">Dupplin Moor</a> (1332) and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Halidon_Hill" title="Battle of Halidon Hill">Halidon Hill</a> (1333), in the face of tough Scottish resistance led by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Andrew_Murray" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Andrew Murray">Sir Andrew Murray</a>, the son of Wallace\'s comrade in arms, successive attempts to secure Balliol on the throne failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Edward III lost interest in the fate of his protege after the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a> with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1341 David was able to return from temporary exile in France. In 1346 under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>, he invaded England in the interests of France, but was defeated and taken prisoner at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Neville%27s_Cross" title="Battle of Neville&#39;s Cross">Battle of Neville\'s Cross</a> on 17 October 1346 and would remain in England as a prisoner for 11 years. His cousin <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert Stewart</a> ruled as guardian in his absence. Balliol finally resigned his claim to the throne to Edward in 1356, before retiring to Yorkshire, where he died in 1364.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Without swearing allegiance to Edward III,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> David was released for a ransom of 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Mark_(money)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mark (money)">marks</a> in 1357, but he was unable to pay, resulting in secret negotiations with the English and attempts to secure succession to the Scottish throne for an English king.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Major issues were his marriages and the failure to produce an heir. His first wife, Joan, the sister of Edward III, left him for England sometime after his return and she died without children in 1362. His planned second marriage to <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Drummond,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret Drummond, Queen of Scotland">Margaret</a>, the widow of the knight Sir John Logie, resulted in a factional division that alienated nobles including Robert Steward. Eventually the king backed the queen\'s opponents and attempted to divorce her. She fled to the continent and appealed to the Pope for support. Before he could marry again David died, apparently unexpectedly, bringing the Bruce dynasty to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_Stewarts_1371.E2.80.931513"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Stewarts_1371\xe2\x80\x931513">The Stewarts 1371\xe2\x80\x931513</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Stewarts 1371\xe2\x80\x931513">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Robert_II.2C_Robert_III_and_James_I"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_II,_Robert_III_and_James_I">Robert II, Robert III and James I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Robert II, Robert III and James I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II of Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III of Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg/170px-King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg/255px-King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg/340px-King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_James_I_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>, who spent much of his life imprisoned in England.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the unexpected death of the childless David II, Robert Stewart, the first of the Stewart (later Stuart) monarchs, came to the throne in 1371. Despite his relatively venerable age of 55, his son, <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">John, Earl of Carrick</a>, grew impatient and assumed the reins of government as Lord Lieutenant. A border incursion into England led to the victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Otterburn" title="Battle of Otterburn">Otterburn</a> in 1388, but at the cost of the life of John\'s ally <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_2nd_Earl_of_Douglas" title="James Douglas, 2nd Earl of Douglas">James Douglas, 2nd Earl of Douglas</a>. This, along with Carrick having suffered a debilitating horse kick, led to a shift in power to his brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany">Robert Stewart, Earl of Fife</a>, who now was appointed as Lieutenant in his place. When Robert II died in 1390 John took the <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III</a>, to avoid awkward questions over the exact status of the first King John, but power rested with his brother Robert, now Duke of Albany.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> After the suspicious death of his elder son, David, Duke of Rothesay in 1402, Robert, fearful for the safety of his younger son, James (the future <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>), sent him to France in 1406. However, the English captured him en route and he spent the next 18 years as a prisoner held for ransom. As a result, after the death of Robert III later that year, regents ruled Scotland: first Albany and after his death in 1420 his son <a href="/wiki/Murdoch_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Murdoch Stewart, Duke of Albany">Murdoch</a>, during whose term of office the country suffered considerable unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nWhen the Scots finally began the ransom payments in 1424, James, aged 32, returned with his English bride, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Beaufort,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Joan Beaufort, Queen of Scots">Joan Beaufort</a>, determined to assert this authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He revoked grants from customs and of lands made during his captivity, undermining the position of those who had gained in his absence, particularly the Albany Stewarts. James had Murdoch and two of his sons tried and then executed with further enforcement of his authority by more arrests and forfeiture of lands. In 1436 he attempted to regain one of the major border fortresses still in English hands at <a href="/wiki/Roxburgh" title="Roxburgh">Roxburgh</a>, but the siege ended in a humiliating defeat. He was murdered by discontented council member Robert Graham and his co-conspirators near the <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars,_Perth" title="Blackfriars, Perth">Blackfriars church, Perth</a> in 1437.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="James_II">James II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: James II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/James_II_of_Scotland.jpg/170px-James_II_of_Scotland.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/James_II_of_Scotland.jpg/255px-James_II_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/James_II_of_Scotland.jpg/340px-James_II_of_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="849" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A later portrait of <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a>, whose eventual military success was ended by his accidental death.</div></div></div>\t\n<p>The assassination left the king\'s seven-year-old son to reign as <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a>. After the execution of a number of suspected conspirators, leadership fell to <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Douglas,_5th_Earl_of_Douglas" title="Archibald Douglas, 5th Earl of Douglas">Archibald Douglas, 5th Earl of Douglas</a>, as lieutenant-general of the realm. After his death in 1439, power was shared uneasily between the Douglas family, <a href="/wiki/William_Crichton,_1st_Lord_Crichton" title="William Crichton, 1st Lord Crichton">William, 1st Lord Crichton</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Lord_Chancellors_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Lord Chancellors of Scotland">Lord Chancellor of Scotland</a> and Sir Alexander Livingston of Callendar. A conspiracy to break the power of the Douglas family led to the "Black Dinner" at <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle" title="Edinburgh Castle">Edinburgh Castle</a> in 1440, which saw the judicial murder of the young <a href="/wiki/William_Douglas,_6th_Earl_of_Douglas" title="William Douglas, 6th Earl of Douglas">William Douglas, 6th Earl of Douglas</a> and his brother by Livingstone and Crichton. The main beneficiary was the victims\' great uncle <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_7th_Earl_of_Douglas" title="James Douglas, 7th Earl of Douglas">James Douglas, Earl of Avondale</a> who became the 7th Earl of Douglas and emerged as the main power in the government.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellp160-9_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellp160-9-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1449 James II was declared to have reached his majority, but the Douglases consolidated their position and the king began a long struggle for power, leading to the murder of the 8th Earl of Douglas at Stirling Castle on 22 February 1452. This opened an intermittent civil war as James attempted to seize Douglas lands, punctuated by a series of humiliating reversals. Gradually James managed to win over the allies of the Douglases with offers of lands, titles and offices and the Douglases\' forces were finally defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arkinholm" title="Battle of Arkinholm">Battle of Arkinholm</a> on 12 May 1455.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellp160-9_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellp160-9-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Once independent, James II proved to be an active and interventionist king. He travelled the country dispensing justice and some of the unpopular policies of the following reign, such as the sale of pardons, may have originated in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Ambitious plans to take Orkney, Shetland and the Isle of Man came to nothing. His attempt to take Roxburgh from the English in 1460 succeeded, but at the cost of his life as he was killed by an exploding artillery piece.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrellp160-9_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrellp160-9-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="James_III">James III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: James III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_III_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/James_III_of_Scotland.jpg/170px-James_III_of_Scotland.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/James_III_of_Scotland.jpg/255px-James_III_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/James_III_of_Scotland.jpg/340px-James_III_of_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_III_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> whose faction riven reign ended in his murder.</div></div></div>\n<p>James II\'s son, aged nine or ten, became king as <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a>, and his widow <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Gueldres" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary of Gueldres">Mary of Gueldres</a> acted as regent until her own death three years later. The Boyd family, led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyd,_1st_Lord_Boyd" title="Robert Boyd, 1st Lord Boyd">Robert, Lord Boyd</a>, emerged as the leading force in the government, making themselves unpopular through self-aggrandisement, with Lord Robert\'s son <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Boyd,_1st_Earl_of_Arran" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Boyd, 1st Earl of Arran">Thomas</a> being made <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Arran" title="Earl of Arran">Earl of Arran</a> and marrying the king\'s sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Stewart,_Princess_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Stewart, Princess of Scotland">Mary</a>. While Robert and Thomas were out of the country in 1469 the king asserted his control, executing members of the Boyd family.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> His foreign policy included a rapprochement with England, with his eldest son, the future <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a>, being betrothed to <a href="/wiki/Cecily_of_York" title="Cecily of York">Cecily of York</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward IV of England</a>, a change of policy that was immensely unpopular at home.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991p6_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991p6-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nDuring the 1470s conflict developed between the king and his brothers, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stewart,_1st_Duke_of_Albany" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Stewart, 1st Duke of Albany">Alexander, Duke of Albany</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Stewart,_Earl_of_Mar_(d._1479)" title="John Stewart, Earl of Mar (d. 1479)">John, Earl of Mar</a>. Mar died suspiciously in 1480 and his estates were forfeited and possibly given to a royal <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cochrane_(architect)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Cochrane (architect)">Robert Cochrane</a>. Albany fled to France in 1479, accused of <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>. By this point the alliance with England was failing and from 1480 there was intermittent war, followed by a <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Berwick_(1482)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capture of Berwick (1482)">full-scale invasion</a> of Scotland two years later, led by the Duke of Gloucester, the future <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a>, and accompanied by Albany. James was imprisoned by his own subjects in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle" title="Edinburgh Castle">Edinburgh Castle</a>, and Albany was established as lieutenant-general. Having taken <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick-upon-Tweed</a> the English retreated and Albany\'s government began to collapse forcing him to flee. Despite conspiracies and more attempts at invasion, James was able to regain power. However, the king managed to alienate the barons, refusing to travel for the implementation of justice, preferring to be resident in Edinburgh, he debased the coinage, probably creating a financial crisis, he continued to pursue an English alliance and dismissed key supporters, including his Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Colin_Campbell,_1st_Earl_of_Argyll" title="Colin Campbell, 1st Earl of Argyll">Colin Campbell, 1st Earl of Argyll</a>, becoming estranged from his wife, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret of Denmark</a>, and his son James. Matters came to a head in 1488 when he faced an army raised by the disaffected nobles, and many former councillors, acting in the name of the prince as James IV. He was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sauchieburn" title="Battle of Sauchieburn">Battle of Sauchieburn</a> and killed.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="James_IV">James IV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: James IV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg/170px-James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg/255px-James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg/340px-James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="690" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_IV_of_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a>, one of the most successful late medieval kings until his death at Flodden.</div></div></div>\n<p>James IV was 15 when he came to the throne, but soon proved a capable and independent minded ruler, whose reign is often considered to have seen a flowering of Scottish culture under the influence of the European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> He took a direct interest in the administration of justice and frequently moved his court in legal <a href="/wiki/Circuit_court" title="Circuit court">circuits</a> of justice ayres.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp14-5_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp14-5-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> He defeated a major Northern rebellion, mainly of supporters of the murdered James III. It began in <a href="/wiki/Dunbarton" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunbarton">Dunbarton</a> in 1489, led by the <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Stewart,_2nd_Earl_of_Lennox" title="Matthew Stewart, 2nd Earl of Lennox">Earl of Lennox</a> and Lord Lyle and spreading through the North.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> James is credited with finally bringing the <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lordship of the Isles</a> under control. He forced through the forfeiture of the lands of the last lord <a href="/wiki/John_of_Islay,_Earl_of_Ross" title="John of Islay, Earl of Ross">John MacDonald</a> in 1493, backing <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Gordon,_3rd_Earl_of_Huntly" title="Alexander Gordon, 3rd Earl of Huntly">Alexander Gordon, 3rd Earl of Huntly</a>\'s power in the region and launching a series of naval campaigns and sieges that resulted in the capture or exile of his rivals by 1507.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For a time he supported <a href="/wiki/Perkin_Warbeck" title="Perkin Warbeck">Perkin Warbeck</a>, the pretender to the English throne, and carried out a brief invasion of England on his behalf in 1496. However, he then established good diplomatic relations with England, and in 1502 signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perpetual_Peace" title="Treaty of Perpetual Peace">Treaty of Perpetual Peace</a>, marrying <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII\'s</a> daughter, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>, thus laying the foundation for the 17th century <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Animosity with <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> helped prompt the renewal of the Auld Alliance in 1512. When the Pope organised a <a href="/wiki/Holy_League_(1511)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy League (1511)">Holy League</a>, which included England, against the French in 1511, James was caught between incompatible diplomatic policies. He tried to suggest an unrealistic European Crusade to Constantinople, but after border skirmishing, when the French were attacked by the English he declared war on England and was excommunicated by the Pope. He sent his navy and gunners to support the French and in 1513 led a major army of perhaps 34,000 over the border.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp7_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp7-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> After using his formidable artillery train to take <a href="/wiki/Norham_Castle" title="Norham Castle">Norham Castle</a> he marched south, where the invasion was stopped decisively on 9 September 1513 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Battle of Flodden</a>. The King, many of his nobles, and a large number of ordinary troops were killed, commemorated by the song "<a href="/wiki/Flowers_of_the_Forest" title="Flowers of the Forest">The Floo\'ers o\' the Forest</a>". Once again Scotland\'s government lay in the hands of regents in the name of the infant <a href="/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland" title="James V of Scotland">James V</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Geography of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_(Location)_Named_(HR).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png/170px-Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png" width="170" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png/255px-Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png/340px-Scotland_%28Location%29_Named_%28HR%29.png 2x" data-file-width="455" data-file-height="759" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_(Location)_Named_(HR).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The topography of Scotland.</div></div></div>\n<p>The defining factor in the geography of Scotland is the distinction between the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> in the north and west and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_lowlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish lowlands">lowlands</a> in the south and east. The highlands are further divided into the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Highlands" title="Northwest Highlands">Northwest Highlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Grampian_Mountains" title="Grampian Mountains">Grampian Mountains</a> by the fault line of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Glen" title="Great Glen">Great Glen</a>. The lowlands are divided into the fertile belt of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Lowlands" title="Central Lowlands">Central Lowlands</a> and the higher terrain of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Uplands" title="Southern Uplands">Southern Uplands</a>, which included the <a href="/wiki/Cheviot_hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheviot hills">Cheviot hills</a>, over which the border with England came to run by the end of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The Central Lowland belt averages about 50 miles in width<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and, because it contains most of the good quality agricultural land and has easier communications, could support most of the urbanisation and elements of conventional medieval government.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp39-40_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp39-40-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> However, the Southern Uplands, and particularly the Highlands were economically less productive and much more difficult to govern. This provided Scotland with a form of protection, as minor English incursions had to cross the difficult southern uplands<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and the two major attempts at conquest by the English, under Edward I and then Edward III, were unable to penetrate the highlands, from which area potential resistance could reconquer the lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, it also made those areas problematic to govern for Scottish kings and much of the political history of the era after the wars of independence circulated around attempts to resolve problems of entrenched localism in these regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp39-40_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp39-40-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was in the later medieval era that the borders of Scotland reached approximately their modern extent. The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> fell under English control in the 14th century, despite several attempts to restore Scottish authority.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The English were able to annexe a large slice of the lowlands under Edward III, but these losses were gradually regained, particularly while England was preoccupied with the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> (1455\xe2\x80\x9385).<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1468 the last great acquisition of Scottish territory occurred when <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret of Denmark</a>, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a> in payment of her dowry.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1482 Berwick, a border fortress and the largest port in medieval Scotland, fell to the English once again, for what was to be the final change of hands.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006p21-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historical_demography_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical demography of Scotland">Historical demography of Scotland</a></div>\n<p>Because medieval Scotland lacked the intrusive government and growing bureaucracy that can be found in contemporaneous England, there is very little evidence on which to base reliable estimates of population before the early 18th century. On the basis that it had roughly a sixth of the farmable land of England, it has been suggested that the population would have been of a similar proportion, probably a little less than a million at its height before the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> reached the country in 1349. Although there is no reliable documentation on the impact of the plague, there are many anecdotal references to abandoned land in the following decades. If the pattern followed that in England, then the population may have fallen to as low as half a million by the end of the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003p109-11_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003p109-11-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Compared with the situation after the redistribution of population in the later <a href="/wiki/Highland_clearances" class="mw-redirect" title="Highland clearances">clearances</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>, these numbers would have been relatively evenly spread over the kingdom, with roughly half living north of the Tay.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps ten per cent of the population lived in one of fifty burghs that existed at the beginning of the period, mainly in the east and south. It has been suggested that they would have had a mean population of about 2,000, but many would be much smaller than 1,000 and the largest, Edinburgh, probably had a population of over 10,000 by the end of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages">Economy of Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg/170px-14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg" width="170" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg/255px-14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg/340px-14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:14thC_Scottish_Economy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The economy of Scotland in the 14th century.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Scotland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Agriculture in Scotland in the Middle Ages">Agriculture in Scotland in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<p>Scotland is roughly half the size of England and Wales, but has only between a fifth and a sixth of the amount of the arable or good pastoral land, making marginal pastoral farming and, with its extensive coastline, fishing, the key factors in the medieval economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gemmill&amp;Mayhew1995pp8-10-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> With difficult terrain, poor roads and methods of transport there was little trade between different areas of the country and most settlements depended on what was produced locally, often with very little in reserve in bad years. Most farming was based on the lowland farmtoun or highland <a href="/wiki/Township_(Scotland)" title="Township (Scotland)">baile</a>, settlements of a handful of families that jointly farmed an area notionally suitable for two or three plough teams, allocated in <a href="/wiki/Run_rig" title="Run rig">run rigs</a> to tenant farmers. They usually ran downhill so that they included both wet and dry land, helping to offset some of the problems of extreme weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This land was divided into the infield, which was in continuous arable cultivation, and the outfield which was rotated between arable and grass.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  Most ploughing was done with a heavy wooden plough with an iron <a href="/wiki/Coulter_(agriculture)" title="Coulter (agriculture)">coulter</a>, pulled by oxen, who were more effective and cheaper to feed than horses. Obligations to the local lord usually included supplying oxen for ploughing the lord\'s land on an annual basis and the much resented obligation to grind corn at the lord\'s mill.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The rural economy appears to have boomed in the 13th century and in the immediate aftermath of the Black Death was still buoyant, but by the 1360s there was a severe falling off of incomes, which can be seen in clerical benefices, of between a third and half compared with the beginning of the era. This was followed by a slow recovery in the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003pp111-16_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003pp111-16-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burghs">Burghs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Burghs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">Burgh</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg/220px-Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg/330px-Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg/440px-Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Provand%27s_Lordship_Castle_Street_Glasgow_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1517876.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Provand\'s Lordship, Glasgow, the only house to survive from the medieval burgh of Glasgow.</div></div></div>\n<p>Most of the burghs were on the east coast, and among them were the largest and wealthiest, including Aberdeen, Perth and Edinburgh, whose growth was facilitated by trade with the continent. Although in the southwest <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> was beginning to develop and <a href="/wiki/Ayr" title="Ayr">Ayr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kirkcudbright" title="Kirkcudbright">Kirkcudbright</a> had occasional links with Spain and France, sea trade with Ireland was much less profitable. In addition to the major <a href="/wiki/Royal_burgh" title="Royal burgh">royal burghs</a> this era saw the proliferation of lesser <a href="/wiki/Burgh_of_barony" title="Burgh of barony">baronial</a> and ecclesiastical burghs, with 51 being created between 1450 and 1516. Most of these were much smaller than their royal counterparts; excluded from international trade, they mainly acted as local markets and centres of craftsmanship.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In general burghs probably carried out far more local trading with their hinterlands, relying on them for food and raw materials. The wool trade was a major export at the beginning of the period, but the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Mange" title="Mange">sheep-scab</a> was a serious blow to the trade and it began to decline as an export from the early 15th century and despite a levelling off, there was another drop in exports as the markets collapsed in the early-16th century Low Countries. Unlike in England, this did not prompt the Scots to turn to large scale cloth production and only poor quality rough cloths seem to have been significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Crafts.2C_industry_and_trade"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crafts,_industry_and_trade">Crafts, industry and trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Crafts, industry and trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_trade_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish trade in the Middle Ages">Scottish trade in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<p>There were relatively few developed crafts in Scotland in this period, although by the later 15th century there were the beginnings of a native iron casting industry, which led to the production of cannon, and of the <a href="/wiki/Silversmith" title="Silversmith">silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goldsmith" title="Goldsmith">goldsmithing</a> for which the country would later be known. As a result, the most important exports were unprocessed raw materials, including wool, hides, salt, fish, animals and coal, while Scotland remained frequently short of wood, iron and, in years of bad harvests, grain.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Exports of hides and particularly salmon, where the Scots held a decisive advantage in quality over their rivals, appear to have held up much better than wool, despite the general economic downturn in Europe in the aftermath of the plague.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003pp111-16_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003pp111-16-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The growing desire among the court, lords, upper clergy and wealthier merchants for luxury goods that largely had to be imported led to a chronic shortage of <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a>. This, and perennial problems in royal finance, led to several debasements of the coinage, with the amount of silver in a penny being cut to almost a fifth between the late 14th century and the late 15th century. The heavily debased "black money" introduced in 1480 had to be withdrawn two years later and may have helped fuel a financial and political crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp41-55_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp41-55-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_society_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Scottish society in the Middle Ages">Scottish society in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_clan_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/170px-Scottish_clan_map.png" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/255px-Scottish_clan_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Scottish_clan_map.png/340px-Scottish_clan_map.png 2x" data-file-width="1065" data-file-height="1400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_clan_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing highland clans and lowland surnames.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinship_and_clans">Kinship and clans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kinship and clans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_surnames" title="Scottish surnames">Scottish surnames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clans" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish clans">Scottish clans</a></div>\n<p>The fundamental social bond in late medieval Scottish society was that of kinship. Descent was <a href="/wiki/Agnatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnatic">agnatic</a>, with members of a group sharing a (sometimes fictional) common ancestor, in the south often reflected in a common surname. Unlike in England, where kinship was predominately <a href="/wiki/Cognatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognatic">cognatic</a> (derived through both males and females), women retained their original surname at marriage and marriages were intended to create friendship between kin groups, rather than a new bond of kinship.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp29-35_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp29-35-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> As a result, a shared surname has been seen as a "test of kinship", proving large bodies of kin who could call on each other\xe2\x80\x99s support and this could help intensify the idea of the feud, which was usually carried out as a form of revenge for a kinsman and for which a large bodies of kin could be counted on to support rival sides, although conflict between members of kin groups also occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combination of agnatic kinship and a feudal system of obligation has been seen as creating the highland <a href="/wiki/Clan" title="Clan">clan</a> system, evident in records from the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Surnames were rare in the highlands until the 17th and 18th centuries and in the Middle Ages all members of a clan did not share a name and most ordinary members were usually not related to its head.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The head of a clan in the beginning of the era was often the strongest male in the main sept or branch of the clan, but later, as <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> began to dominate, was usually the eldest son of the last chief.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts2000p13_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts2000p13-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The leading families of a clan formed the <i>fine</i>, often seen as equivalent to lowland gentlemen, providing council in peace and leadership in war,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and below them were the <i>daoine usisle</i> (in Gaelic) or <a href="/wiki/Tacksmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Tacksmen">tacksmen</a> (in Scots), who managed the clan lands and collected the rents.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In the isles and along the adjacent western seaboard there were also <i>buannachann</i>, who acted as a military elite, defending the clan lands from raids or taking part in attacks on clan enemies. Most of the followers of the clan were tenants, who supplied labour to the clan heads and sometimes act as soldiers. In the early modern era they would take the clan name as their surname, turning the clan into a massive, if often fictive, kin group.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts2000p13_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts2000p13-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_structure">Social structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Social structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LateMedievalScotsRanks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/LateMedievalScotsRanks.jpg/300px-LateMedievalScotsRanks.jpg" width="300" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/LateMedievalScotsRanks.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="436" data-file-height="155" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LateMedievalScotsRanks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A table of ranks in late Medieval Scottish society.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the late medieval era the terminology used to describe the different ranks of Scottish social structure was increasingly dominated by the Scots language and as a result began to parallel the terminology used in England. This consciousness over status was reflected in military and (from 1430) <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumptuary">sumptuary</a> legislation, which set out the types of weapons and armour that should be maintained, and clothes that could be worn, by various ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp29-35_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp29-35-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Below the king were a small number of <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">dukes</a> (usually descended from very close relatives of the king) and <a href="/wiki/List_of_earls#Earls_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="List of earls">earls</a>, who formed the senior nobility. Below them were the barons, and, from the 1440s, fulfilling the same role were the <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Lords of Parliament">lords of Parliament</a>, the lowest level of the nobility with the rank-given right to attend the Estates. There were perhaps 40 to 60 of these in Scotland throughout the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2000_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2000-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Members of these noble ranks, perhaps particularly those that had performed military or administrative service to the Crown, might also be eligible for the status of knighthood.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Below these were the <a href="/wiki/Laird" title="Laird">lairds</a>, roughly equivalent to the English <a href="/wiki/Gentlemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Gentlemen">gentlemen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2000_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2000-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Most were in some sense in the service of the major nobility, either in terms of land or military obligations,<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2000_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2000-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> roughly half sharing with them their name and a distant and often uncertain form of kinship.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodacre1999pp57-60_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodacre1999pp57-60-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Serfdom died out in Scotland in the 14th century, although through the system of <a href="/wiki/Court_baron" class="mw-redirect" title="Court baron">courts baron</a> landlords still exerted considerable control over their tenants.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodacre1999pp57-60_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodacre1999pp57-60-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Below the lords and lairds were a variety of groups, often ill-defined. These included <a href="/wiki/Yeomen" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeomen">yeomen</a>, sometimes called "bonnet lairds", often owning substantial land, and below them the <a href="/wiki/Husbandmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Husbandmen">husbandmen</a>, lesser landholders and free tenants that made up the majority of the working population.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Society in the burghs was headed by wealthier merchants, who often held local office as a <a href="/wiki/Burgess_(title)" title="Burgess (title)">burgess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">alderman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bailie" title="Bailie">bailies</a> or as a member of the council. A small number of these successful merchants were dubbed knights for their service by the king by the end of the era, although this seems to have been an exceptional form of civic knighthood that did not put them on a par with landed knights.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Below them were <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">craftsmen</a> and workers that made up the majority of the urban population.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp48-9_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp48-9-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_conflict">Social conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Social conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historians have noted considerable political conflict in the burghs between the great merchants and craftsmen throughout the period. Merchants attempted to prevent lower crafts and <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">gilds</a> from infringing on their trade, monopolies and political power. Craftsmen attempted to emphasise their importance and to break into disputed areas of economic activity, setting prices and standards of workmanship. In the 15th century a series of statutes cemented the political position of the merchants, with limitations on the ability of residents to influence the composition of burgh councils and many of the functions of regulation taken on by the bailies.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp48-9_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp48-9-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In rural society historians have noted a lack of evidence of widespread unrest similar to that evidenced the <a href="/wiki/Jacquerie" title="Jacquerie">Jacquerie</a> of 1358 in France and the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> of 1381 in England, possibly because there was relatively little of the type of change in agriculture, like the <a href="/wiki/Enclosure" title="Enclosure">enclosure</a> of common land, that could create widespread resentment before the modern era. Instead a major factor was the willingness of tenants to support their betters in any conflict in which they were involved, for which landlords reciprocated with charity and support.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp50-1_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp50-1-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Highland and border society acquired a reputation for lawless activity, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Feud" title="Feud">feud</a>. However, more recent interpretations have pointed to the feud as a means of preventing and speedily resolving disputes by forcing arbitration, compensation and resolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp28&amp;35-9_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp28&amp;35-9-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_in_Medieval_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Government in Medieval Scotland">Government in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_II_(Alba)_i.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG/220px-Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG/330px-Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG/440px-Robert_II_%28Alba%29_i.JPG 2x" data-file-width="854" data-file-height="814" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_II_(Alba)_i.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An engraving of <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II</a> depicted enthroned as a lawgiver on his great seal.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Crown">The Crown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The Crown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Crown was at the centre of government in late medieval Scotland. The unification of the kingdom, the spread of Anglo-Norman custom, the development of a European trading economy and Robert I\'s success in achieving independence from England, all did much to build up the prestige of the institution.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> However, its authority within the kingdom was not unchallenged, not least from the many semi-independent lordships and it endured a series of crisis, particularly frequent minorities and resulting regencies. All of this, in addition to the relative poverty of the kingdom and the lack of a system of regular taxation, helped to limit the scale of central administration and government.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp14-5_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp14-5-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Much more than the English monarchy, the Scottish court remained a largely itinerant institution, with the king moving between royal castles, particularly Perth and Stirling, but also holding judicial sessions throughout the kingdom, with Edinburgh only beginning to emerge as the capital in the reign of James III at the cost of considerable unpopularity.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp14-5_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp14-5-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Like most western European monarchies, the Scottish Crown in the 15th century adopted the example of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">Burgundian court</a>, through formality and elegance putting itself at the centre of culture and political life, defined with display, ritual and pageantry, reflected in elaborate new palaces and patronage of the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991p18_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991p18-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privy_Council">Privy Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Privy Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_Scotland" title="Privy Council of Scotland">Privy Council of Scotland</a></div>\n<p>After the Crown the most important government institution was the Privy Council, composed of the king\'s closest advisers, but which, unlike in England, retained legislative and judicial powers. It was relatively small, with normally less than 10 members in a meeting, some of whom were nominated by Parliament, particularly during the many minorities of the era, as a means of limiting the power of a regent.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp22-3_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp22-3-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The council was a virtually full-time institution by the late 15th century, and surviving records from the period indicate it was critical in the working of royal justice. Nominally members of the council were some of the great magnates of the realm, but they rarely attended meetings. Most of the active members of the council for most of the period were career administrators and lawyers, almost exclusively university-educated clergy, the most successful of which moved on to occupy the major ecclesiastical positions in the realm as bishops and, towards the end of the period, archbishops. By the end of the 15th century this group was being joined by increasing numbers of literate laymen, often secular lawyers, of which the most successful gained preferment in the judicial system and grants of lands and lordships. From the reign of James III onwards the clerically-dominated post of <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor_of_Scotland" title="Lord Chancellor of Scotland">Lord Chancellor</a> was increasingly taken by leading laymen.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp22-3_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp22-3-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliament">Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg/220px-The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg/330px-The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg/440px-The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="378" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Old_Tolbooth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Old_Tolbooth,_Edinburgh" title="Old Tolbooth, Edinburgh">Old Tolbooth, Edinburgh</a>, location of Scottish Parliaments from the mid-15th century to the mid 17th century.</div></div></div><p>\nThe next most important body in the process of government was parliament, which had evolved by the late 13th century from the King\'s Council of Bishops and Earls into a \'colloquium\' with a political and judicial role.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> By the early 14th century, the attendance of <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fee_simple" title="Fee simple">freeholders</a> had become important, and probably from 1326 <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burgh</a> commissioners joined them to form the <a href="/wiki/Three_Estates" class="mw-redirect" title="Three Estates">Three Estates</a>, meeting in a variety of major towns throughout the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It acquired significant powers over particular issues, including
