consent for taxation, but it also had a strong influence over justice, foreign policy, war, and other legislation, whether political, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical">ecclesiastical</a>, social or economic. From the early 1450s, a great deal of the legislative business of the Scottish Parliament was usually carried out by a parliamentary committee known as the \'Lords of the Articles\', chosen by the three estates to draft legislation which was then presented to the full assembly to be confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Parliamentary business was also carried out by \'sister\' institutions, before c. 1500 by <a href="/wiki/General_Council_of_Scotland" title="General Council of Scotland">General Council</a> and thereafter by the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Estates_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention of Estates of Scotland">Convention of Estates</a>. These could carry out much business also dealt with by Parliament\xe2\x80\x94taxation, legislation and policy-making\xe2\x80\x94but lacked the ultimate authority of a full parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  In the 15th century parliament was being called on an almost annual basis, more often than its English counterpart, and was willing to offer occasional resistance or criticism to the policies of the Crown, particular in the unpopular reign of James III.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991p18_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991p18-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, from about 1494, after his success against the Stewarts and Douglases and over rebels in 1482 and 1488, James IV managed to largely dispense with the institution and it might have declined, like many other systems of Estates in continental Europe, had it not been for his death in 1513 and another long minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991p21_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991p21-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_local_government_in_Scotland" title="History of local government in Scotland">History of local government in Scotland</a></div>\n<p>At a local level, government combined traditional kinship-based lordships with a relatively small system of royal offices. Until the 15th century the ancient pattern of major lordships survived largely intact, with the addition of two new "scattered earldoms" of <a href="/wiki/Clan_Douglas" title="Clan Douglas">Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clan_Crawford" title="Clan Crawford">Crawford</a>, thanks to royal patronage after the Wars of Independence, mainly in the borders and south-west. The dominant kindred were the Stewarts, who came to control many of the earldoms. Their acquisition of the Crown, and a series of internal conflicts and confiscations, meant that by around the 1460s the monarchy had transformed its position within the realm, gaining control of most of the "provincial" earldoms and lordships. Rather than running semi-independent lordships, the major magnates now had scattered estates and occasional regions of major influence. In the lowlands the Crown was now able to administer government through a system of sheriffdoms and other appointed officers, rather than semi-independent lordships. In the highlands James II created two new provincial earldoms for his favourites: Argyll for the <a href="/wiki/Clan_Campbell" title="Clan Campbell">Campbells</a> and Huntly for the <a href="/wiki/Clan_Gordon" title="Clan Gordon">Gordons</a>, which acted as a bulwark against the vast <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lordship of the Isles</a> built up by the <a href="/wiki/Clan_Donald" title="Clan Donald">Macdonalds</a>. James IV largely resolved the Macdonald problem by annexing the estates and titles of <a href="/wiki/John_Macdonald_II" class="mw-redirect" title="John Macdonald II">John Macdonald II</a> to the Crown in 1493 after discovering his plans for an alliance with the English.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Warfare_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Warfare in Medieval Scotland">Warfare in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armies">Armies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Armies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Otterburn_Battle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/180px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg" width="180" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/270px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Otterburn_Battle.jpg/360px-Otterburn_Battle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1926" data-file-height="1950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Otterburn_Battle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Otterburn" class="mw-redirect" title="The Battle of Otterburn">The Battle of Otterburn</a> (1388) in a miniature from <a href="/wiki/Jean_Froissart" title="Jean Froissart">Jean Froissart</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Froissart%27s_Chronicles" title="Froissart&#39;s Chronicles">Chroniques</a></i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scottish armies of the late medieval era depended on a combination of familial, communal and <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> forms of service. "Scottish service" (<i>servitum Scoticanum</i>), also known as "common service" (<i>communis exertcitus</i>), a levy of all able-bodied freemen aged between 16 and 60, provided the bulk of armed forces, with (according to decrees) 8 days warning. Feudal obligations, by which knights held castles and estates in exchange for service, provided troops on a 40-day basis. By the second half of the 14th century money contracts of <i>bonds</i> or <i>bands of <a href="/wiki/Manrent" title="Manrent">manrent</a></i>, similar to English <a href="/wiki/Indentures" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentures">indentures</a> of the same period, were being used to retain more professional troops, particularly <a href="/wiki/Men-at-arms" class="mw-redirect" title="Men-at-arms">men-at-arms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2004_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2004-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In practice forms of service tended to blur and overlap and several major Scottish lords brought contingents from their kindred.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2004_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2004-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These systems produced relatively large numbers of poorly armoured infantry, often armed with 12\xe2\x80\x9314 foot spears. They often formed the large close order defensive formations of <a href="/wiki/Shiltron" class="mw-redirect" title="Shiltron">shiltrons</a>, able to counter mounted knights as they did at Bannockburn, but vulnerable to arrows (and later <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> fire) and relatively immobile, as they proved at Halidon Hill.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> There were attempts to replace spears with longer pikes of 15\xc2\xbd to 18\xc2\xbd feet in the later 15th century, in emulation of successes over mounted troops in the Netherlands and Switzerland, but this does not appear to have been successful until the eve of the Flodden campaign in early 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> There were smaller numbers of archers and men-at-arms, which were often outnumbered when facing the English on the battlefield. Archers became much sought after as mercenaries in French armies of the 15th century to help counter the English superiority in this arm, becoming a major element of the French royal guards as the <a href="/wiki/Garde_%C3%89cossaise" title="Garde \xc3\x89cossaise">Garde \xc3\x89cossaise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Scottish men-at-arms often dismounted to fight beside the infantry, with perhaps a small mounted reserve, and it has been suggested that these tactics were copied and refined by the English, leading to their successes in the Hundred Years\' War.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artillery">Artillery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Artillery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mons_Meg,_Medieval_Bombard,_Edinburgh,_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg/220px-Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg/330px-Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg/440px-Mons_Meg%2C_Medieval_Bombard%2C_Edinburgh%2C_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="924" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mons_Meg,_Medieval_Bombard,_Edinburgh,_Scotland._Pic_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mons_Meg" title="Mons Meg">Mons Meg</a> at Edinburgh Castle, with its 20" (50 cm) calibre <a href="/wiki/Round_shot" title="Round shot">cannonballs</a>.</div></div></div><p>\nThe Stewarts attempted to follow France and England in building up an artillery train. The abortive siege of Roxborugh in 1436 under James I was probably the first conflict in which the Scots made serious use of artillery.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> James II had a royal gunner and received gifts of artillery from the continent, including two giant bombards made for <a href="/wiki/Philip_the_Good,_Duke_of_Burgundy" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip the Good, Duke of Burgundy">Philip the Good, Duke of Burgundy</a>, one of which, <a href="/wiki/Mons_Meg" title="Mons Meg">Mons Meg</a>, still survives. Although these were probably already outdated on the continent, they represented impressive military technology when they reached Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> James II enthusiasm for artillery cost him his life,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and James III also experienced ill-fortune when artillery sent from <a href="/wiki/Sigismund,_Archduke_of_Austria" title="Sigismund, Archduke of Austria">Sigismund, Archduke of Austria</a>, sank in a storm en route to Scotland in 1481.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991p19_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991p19-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> James IV brought in experts from France, Germany and the Netherlands and established a foundry in 1511. Edinburgh Castle had a house of artillery where visitors could see cannon cast for what became a formidable train, allowing him to send cannon to France and Ireland and to quickly subdue <a href="/wiki/Norham_Castle" title="Norham Castle">Norham Castle</a> in the Flodden campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> However, 18 heavy artillery pieces had to be drawn by 400 oxen and slowed the advancing army, proving ineffective against the longer range and smaller calibre English guns at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden" title="Battle of Flodden">Battle of Flodden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy">Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots_Navy" title="Royal Scots Navy">Royal Scots Navy</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Michael.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Great_Michael.jpg/180px-Great_Michael.jpg" width="180" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Great_Michael.jpg/270px-Great_Michael.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Great_Michael.jpg/360px-Great_Michael.jpg 2x" data-file-width="435" data-file-height="460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Michael.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of the <i><a href="/wiki/Michael_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael (ship)">Great Michael</a></i>, the largest ship in the world when launched in 1511.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the establishment of Scottish independence, Robert I turned his attention to building up a Scottish naval capacity. This was largely focused on the west coast, with the Exchequer Rolls of 1326 recording the feudal duties of his vassals in that region to aid him with their vessels and crews. Towards the end of his reign he supervised the building of at least one royal man-of-war near his palace at <a href="/wiki/Cardross,_Argyll_and_Bute" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardross, Argyll and Bute">Cardross</a> on the <a href="/wiki/River_Clyde" title="River Clyde">River Clyde</a>. In the late 14th century naval warfare with England was conducted largely by hired Scots, Flemish and French merchantmen and privateers.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> James I took a greater interest in naval power. After his return to Scotland in 1424 he established a shipbuilding yard at <a href="/wiki/Leith" title="Leith">Leith</a>, a house for marine stores, and a workshop. King\'s ships, one of which accompanied him on his expedition to the Islands in 1429, were built and equipped there to be used for trade as well as war, and the office of <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Admiral_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord High Admiral of Scotland">Lord High Admiral</a> was probably founded in this period. In his struggles with his nobles in 1488 James III received assistance from his two warships the <i>Flower</i> and the <i>King\'s Carvel</i> also known as the <i>Yellow Carvel</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>James IV put the enterprise on a new footing, founding a new harbour at <a href="/wiki/Newhaven,_Edinburgh" title="Newhaven, Edinburgh">Newhaven</a> in May 1504, and two years later ordered the construction of a dockyard at the Pools of <a href="/wiki/Airth" title="Airth">Airth</a>. The upper reaches of the Forth were protected by new fortifications on <a href="/wiki/Inchgarvie" title="Inchgarvie">Inchgarvie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The king acquired a total of 38 ships for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scottish_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Scottish Navy">Royal Scottish Navy</a>, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Scottish_warship_Margaret" title="Scottish warship Margaret">Margaret</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">carrack</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Michael_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael (ship)">Michael</a></i> or <i>Great Michael</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smout1992p45_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smout1992p45-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The latter, built at great expense at <a href="/wiki/Newhaven,_Edinburgh" title="Newhaven, Edinburgh">Newhaven</a> and launched in 1511, was 240 feet (73&#160;m) in length, weighed 1,000 tons, had 24 cannon, and was, at that time, the largest ship in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Smout1992p45_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smout1992p45-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Scottish ships had some success against privateers, accompanied the king in his expeditions in the islands and intervened in conflicts in Scandinavia and the Baltic.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In the Flodden campaign the fleet consisted of 16 large and 10 smaller craft. After a raid on Carrickfergus in Ireland, it joined up with the French and had little impact on the war. After the disaster at Flodden the <i>Great Michael</i>, and perhaps other ships, were sold to the French and the king\'s ships disappeared from royal records after 1516.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Christianity in Medieval Scotland">Christianity in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Church">The Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Wardlaw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_Wardlaw.jpg/180px-Henry_Wardlaw.jpg" width="180" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_Wardlaw.jpg/270px-Henry_Wardlaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_Wardlaw.jpg/360px-Henry_Wardlaw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="1442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Wardlaw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Bishop <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wardlaw" title="Henry Wardlaw">Henry Wardlaw</a> (d. 1440), <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_St_Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of St Andrews">Bishop of St Andrews</a>, tutor and advisor to <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/The_University_of_St_Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="The University of St Andrews">The University of St Andrews</a> and one of the key figures in fighting the threat of <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Since gaining its independence from English ecclesiastical organisation in 1192, the Catholic Church in Scotland had been a "special daughter of the see of Rome", enjoying a direct relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Lacking archbishoprics, it was in practice run by special councils of made up of all the bishops, with the bishop of St Andrews emerging as the most important player, until in 1472 St Andrews became the first archbishopric, to be followed by Glasgow in 1492.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Late medieval religion had its political aspects, with Robert I carrying the <i>brecbennoch</i> (or <a href="/wiki/Monymusk_reliquary" class="mw-redirect" title="Monymusk reliquary">Monymusk reliquary</a>), said to contain the remains of <a href="/wiki/St._Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Columba">St. Columba</a>, into battle at Bannockburn<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and James IV using his pilgrimages to <a href="/wiki/Tain" title="Tain">Tain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whithorn" title="Whithorn">Whithorn</a> to help bring Ross and Galloway under royal authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> There were also further attempts to differentiate Scottish liturgical practice from that in England, with a printing press established under royal patent in 1507 to replace the English <a href="/wiki/Sarum_Use" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarum Use">Sarum Use</a> for services.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> As elsewhere in Europe, the collapse of papal authority in the <a href="/wiki/Papal_Schism" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal Schism">Papal Schism</a> allowed the Scottish Crown to gain effective control of major ecclesiastical appointments within the kingdom, a position recognised by the Papacy in 1487. This led to the placement of clients and relatives of the king in key positions, including James IV\'s illegitimate son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stewart_(Archbishop_of_St_Andrews)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Stewart (Archbishop of St Andrews)">Alexander</a>, who was nominated as Archbishop of St. Andrews at the age of 11, intensifying royal influence and also opening the Church to accusations of <a href="/wiki/Venal" class="mw-redirect" title="Venal">venality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, relationships between the Scottish crown and the Papacy were generally good, with James IV receiving tokens of papal favour.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_practice">Popular practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Popular practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_popular_religion_in_Scotland" title="History of popular religion in Scotland">History of popular religion in Scotland</a></div>\n<p>Traditional Protestant historiography tended to stress the corruption and unpopularity of the late medieval Scottish church, but more recent research has indicated the ways in which it met the spiritual needs of different social groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Historians have discerned a decline of monasticism in this period, with many religious houses keeping smaller numbers of monks, and those remaining often abandoning communal living for a more individual and secular lifestyle. New monastic endowments from the nobility also declined in the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrell2000p246_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrell2000p246-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, the burghs saw the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicant</a> orders of <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a> in the later 15th century, who placed an emphasis on preaching and ministering to the population. The order of <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Observant Friars</a> were organised as a Scottish province from 1467 and the older Franciscans and <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a> were recognised as separate provinces in the 1480s.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In most burghs, in contrast to English towns where churches tended to proliferate, there was usually only one parish church,<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> but as the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a> gained in importance in the period, the number of chapelries, priests and masses for the dead within them grew rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrell2000p254_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrell2000p254-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The number of altars to saints also grew dramatically, with <a href="/wiki/Dundee_Parish_Church_(St_Mary%27s)" title="Dundee Parish Church (St Mary&#39;s)">St. Mary\'s in Dundee</a> having perhaps 48 and <a href="/wiki/St_Giles%27_Cathedral" title="St Giles&#39; Cathedral">St Giles\' in Edinburgh</a> over 50,<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> as did the number of saints celebrated in Scotland, with about 90 being added to the <a href="/wiki/Missal" title="Missal">missal</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Kirk_of_St_Nicholas,_Aberdeen" class="mw-redirect" title="Kirk of St Nicholas, Aberdeen">St Nicholas church in Aberdeen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Peters2004p147_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2004p147-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> New cults of devotion connected with Jesus and the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> also began to reach Scotland in the 15th century, including The <a href="/wiki/Five_Wounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Wounds">Five Wounds</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Blood_of_Christ" title="Blood of Christ">Holy Blood</a> and The <a href="/wiki/Holy_Name_of_Jesus" title="Holy Name of Jesus">Holy Name of Jesus</a> and new feasts including <a href="/wiki/The_Presentation" class="mw-redirect" title="The Presentation">The Presentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Visitation_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Visitation (Christianity)">The Visitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dedication_of_Saint_Mary_Major" class="mw-redirect" title="Dedication of Saint Mary Major">Mary of the Snows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9_92-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp26-9-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peters2004p147_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2004p147-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In the early 14th century the Papacy managed to minimise the problem of clerical pluralism, but with relatively poor <a href="/wiki/Benefice" title="Benefice">livings</a> and a shortage of clergy, particularly after the Black Death, in the 15th century the number of clerics holding two or more livings rapidly increased.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrell2000pp244-5_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrell2000pp244-5-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> This meant that parish clergy were largely drawn from the lower and less educated ranks of the profession, leading to frequent complaints about their standards of education or ability, although there is little clear evidence that this was actually declining.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp75-87_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp75-87-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Heresy, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lollard" class="mw-redirect" title="Lollard">Lollardry</a>, began to reach Scotland from England and Bohemia in the early 15th century, but despite evidence of a number of burnings of heretics and some apparent support for its anti-sacramental elements, it probably remained a relatively small movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrell2000p2547_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrell2000p2547-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Education in Medieval Scotland">Education in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College,_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/160px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" width="160" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/240px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg/320px-Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College%2C_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_St._Salvator%27s_College,_St._Andrews_Fife.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tower of St. Salvator\'s College, University of St. Andrews.</div></div></div>\n<p>In medieval Scotland education was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Church</a> and largely aimed at the training and education of clerics. In the later medieval period there was a general increase in the numbers of educational institutions as well as increasing use by the laity. These included private tuition in the families of lords and wealthy burghers, song schools attached to most major churches and an increasing number of grammar schools, particularly in the expanding burghs. These were almost exclusively aimed at boys, but by the end of the 15th century Edinburgh also had schools for girls.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The growing emphasis on education cumulated with the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1496" title="Education Act 1496">Education Act 1496</a>, which decreed that all sons of barons and freeholders of substance should attend grammar schools. All this resulted in an increase in literacy, but which was largely concentrated among a male and wealthy elite,<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> with perhaps 60 per cent of the nobility being literate by the end of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp68-72_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp68-72-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the 15th century those who wished to attend university had to travel to England or the continent, but this situation was transformed by the founding of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> in 1413, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> in 1451 and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> in 1495.<sup id="cite_ref-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bawcutt&amp;Williams2006pp29-30-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Initially these institutions were designed for the training of clerics, but they would increasingly be used by laymen who would begin to challenge the clerical monopoly of administrative post in the government and law. Scottish scholars continued to visit the continent for their second degrees and this international contact helped bring the new ideas of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> back into Scottish intellectual life.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp68-72_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp68-72-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Art in Medieval Scotland">Art in Medieval Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Scotland" title="Architecture of Scotland">Architecture of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Hall,_Stirling_Castle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg/220px-Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg/330px-Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg/440px-Great_Hall%2C_Stirling_Castle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Hall,_Stirling_Castle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Hall of Stirling Castle, built by <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> in the late fifteenth century, showing a combination of traditional Scottish and continental Renaissance features.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scotland is known for its dramatically placed castles, many of which date from the late medieval era. In contrast to England, where the wealthy began to move towards more comfortable grand houses, these continued to be built into the modern period, developing into the style of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Baronial" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Baronial">Scottish Baronial</a> architecture in the 19th century, popular amongst the minor aristocracy and merchant class.<sup id="cite_ref-britbbc_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britbbc-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> This building type, often built with defence in mind in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Tower_house" title="Tower house">tower house</a>, was characterised by <a href="/wiki/Corbel" title="Corbel">corbelled</a> turrets and crow-stepped <a href="/wiki/Gable" title="Gable">gables</a> marked the first uniquely Scottish mode of building.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scottish_renaissance_painted_ceilings" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish renaissance painted ceilings">Ceilings</a> of these houses were decorated with vividly coloured painting on boards and beams, using <a href="/wiki/Emblem" title="Emblem">emblematic</a> motifs from European pattern books or the artist\'s interpretation of trailing grotesque patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The grandest buildings of this type were the royal palaces in this style at <a href="/wiki/Linlithgow_Palace" title="Linlithgow Palace">Linlithgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holyrood_Palace" title="Holyrood Palace">Holyrood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Palace" title="Falkland Palace">Falkland</a> and the remodelled <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> all of which have elements of continental European architecture, particularly from France and the Low Countries, adapted to Scottish idioms and materials (particularly stone and <a href="/wiki/Harl" title="Harl">harl</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Palliser2000p391-2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palliser2000p391-2-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> More modest buildings with continental influences can be seen in the late 15th century western tower of St Mary\'s parish church, Dundee, and tollbooths like the one at Dunbar.<sup id="cite_ref-Palliser2000p391-2_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palliser2000p391-2-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parish church architecture in Scotland was often much less elaborate than in England, with many churches remaining simple oblongs, without <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transepts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aisle" title="Aisle">aisles</a>, and often without towers. In the highlands they were often even simpler, many built of rubble masonry and sometimes indistinguishable from the outside from houses or farm buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> However, there were some churches built in a grander continental style. French master-mason John Morrow was employed at the building of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Cathedral" title="Glasgow Cathedral">Glasgow Cathedral</a> and the rebuilding of <a href="/wiki/Melrose_Abbey" title="Melrose Abbey">Melrose Abbey</a>, both considered fine examples of Gothic architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The interiors of churches were often more elaborate before the Reformation, with highly decorated sacrament houses, like the ones surviving at Deskford and Kinkell.<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The carvings at <a href="/wiki/Rosslyn_Chapel" title="Rosslyn Chapel">Rosslyn Chapel</a>, created in the mid-15th century, elaborately depicting the progression of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_deadly_sins" title="Seven deadly sins">seven deadly sins</a>, are considered some of the finest in the Gothic style.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Late medieval Scottish churches also often contained elaborate burial monuments, like the Douglas tombs in the town of <a href="/wiki/Douglas,_South_Lanarkshire" title="Douglas, South Lanarkshire">Douglas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whyte&amp;Whyte1991p117-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is relatively little information about native Scottish artists during the late Middle Ages. As in England, the monarchy may have had model portraits used for copies and reproductions, but the versions that survive are generally crude by continental standards.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Much more impressive are the works or artists imported from the continent, particularly the Netherlands, generally considered the centre of painting in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Renaissance" title="Northern Renaissance">Northern Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The products of these connections included the delicate hanging lamp in St. John\'s Kirk in <a href="/wiki/Perth,_Scotland" title="Perth, Scotland">Perth</a>; the tabernacles and images of St Catherine and St John brought to <a href="/wiki/Dunkeld" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunkeld">Dunkeld</a>, and vestments and hangings in Holyrood; <a href="/wiki/Hugo_van_Der_Goes" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugo van Der Goes">Hugo van Der Goes</a>\'s altarpiece for the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College_Kirk" title="Trinity College Kirk">Trinity College Church in Edinburgh</a>, commissioned by James III, the work after which the Flemish <a href="/wiki/Master_of_James_IV_of_Scotland" title="Master of James IV of Scotland">Master of James IV of Scotland</a> is named, and the illustrated Flemish <a href="/wiki/Simon_Bening" title="Simon Bening">Bening</a> <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of Hours">Book of Hours</a>, given by James IV to Margaret Tudor.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp57-9_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp57-9-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_literature">Language and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Language and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Scots_language" title="History of the Scots language">History of the Scots language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature" title="Scottish literature">Scottish literature</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg/220px-Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg/330px-Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg/440px-Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Dean_of_Lismore_page.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two facsimiles from <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dean_of_Lismore" title="Book of the Dean of Lismore">Book of the Dean of Lismore</a></i>, printed by <a href="/wiki/William_Forbes_Skene" title="William Forbes Skene">William Forbes Skene</a> in 1862.</div></div></div>\n<p>In was in this period that the Scots language became the dominant language of the state and the social elite, while also becoming linked with Scottish national identity and making inroads into the highland zone at the expense of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Gaelic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a>, often called "English" in this period, was derived largely from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, with the addition of elements from Gaelic and French. Although resembling the language spoken in northern England, it became a distinct dialect from the late 14th century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> It was the dominant language of the lowlands and borders, brought there largely by Anglo-Saxon settlers from the 5th century, but began to be adopted by the ruling elite as they gradually abandoned French in the late medieval era. By the 15th century it was the language of government, with acts of parliament, council records and treasurer\'s accounts almost all using it from the reign of James I onwards. As a result, Gaelic, once dominant north of the Tay, began a steady decline.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaelic was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Bardic_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Bardic tradition">bardic tradition</a>, which provided a mechanism for the transference of oral culture from generation to generation. Members of bardic schools were trained in the complex rules and forms of Gaelic poetry. In a non-literate society they were the repositories of knowledge, including not just stories and songs, but also genealogies and medicine. They were found in many of the courts of the great lords, down to the chiefdoms of the highlands at the beginning of the period. The bardic tradition was not completely isolated from trends elsewhere, including love poetry influenced by continental developments and medical manuscripts from Padua, Salerno and Montpellier translated from Latin. The Gaelic oral tradition also began to manifest itself in written form, with the great compilation of Gaelic poetry, the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dean_of_Lismore" title="Book of the Dean of Lismore">Book of the Dean of Lismore</a></i> produced by James and Duncan MacGregor at the beginning of the 16th century, probably designed for use in the courts of the greater chiefs. However, by the 15th century lowland writers were beginning to treat Gaelic as a second class, rustic and even amusing language, helping to frame attitudes towards the highlands and to create a cultural gulf with the lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was Scots that emerged as the language of national literature in Scotland. The first surviving major text is <a href="/wiki/John_Barbour_(poet)" title="John Barbour (poet)">John Barbour</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brus" title="The Brus">Brus</a></i> (1375), composed under the patronage of Robert II and telling the story in epic poetry of Robert I\'s actions before the English invasion until the end of the war of independence.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The work was extremely popular among the Scots-speaking aristocracy and Barbour is referred to as the father of Scots poetry, holding a similar place to his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Chaucer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaucer">Chaucer</a> in England.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the early 15th century these were followed by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_of_Wyntoun" title="Andrew of Wyntoun">Andrew of Wyntoun</a>\'s verse <i>Orygynale Cronykil of Scotland</i> and <a href="/wiki/Blind_Harry" title="Blind Harry">Blind Harry</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Actes_and_Deidis_of_the_Illustre_and_Vallyeant_Campioun_Schir_William_Wallace" class="mw-redirect" title="The Actes and Deidis of the Illustre and Vallyeant Campioun Schir William Wallace">The Wallace</a></i>, which blended <a href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance (heroic literature)">historical romance</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">verse chronicle</a>. They were probably influenced by Scots versions of popular French romances that were also produced in the period, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Buik_of_Alexander" title="The Buik of Alexander">The Buik of Alexander</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lancelot" title="Lancelot">Launcelot o the Laik</a></i> and <i>The Porteous of Noblenes</i> by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Hay_(poet)" title="Gilbert Hay (poet)">Gibert Hay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg/220px-Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg/330px-Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg/440px-Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="618" data-file-height="562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Gavin_Douglas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seal of <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a>, Bishop of Dunkeld, makar and translator.</div></div></div>\n<p>Much Middle Scots literature was produced by <a href="/wiki/Makars" class="mw-redirect" title="Makars">makars</a>, poets with links to the royal court. These included <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a> who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kingis_Quair" title="The Kingis Quair">The Kingis Quair</a></i>. Many of the makars had university education and so were also connected with the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesia (church)">Kirk</a>. However, Dunbar\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lament_for_the_Makaris" title="Lament for the Makaris">Lament for the Makaris</a></i> (c. 1505) provides evidence of a wider tradition of secular writing outside of Court and Kirk now largely lost.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Before the advent of printing in Scotland, writers such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Henryson" title="Robert Henryson">Robert Henryson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar" title="William Dunbar">William Dunbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kennedy_(poet)" title="Walter Kennedy (poet)">Walter Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a> have been seen as leading a golden age in Scottish poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 15th century, Scots prose also began to develop as a genre. Although there are earlier fragments of original Scots prose, such as the <i>Auchinleck Chronicle</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> the first complete surviving works include <a href="/wiki/John_Ireland_(theologian)" title="John Ireland (theologian)">John Ireland</a>\'s <i>The Meroure of Wyssdome</i> (1490).<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> There were also prose translations of French books of chivalry that survive from the 1450s, including <i>The Book of the Law of Armys</i> and the <i>Order of Knychthode</i> and the treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Secretum_Secretorum" title="Secretum Secretorum">Secreta Secetorum</a></i>, an Arabic work believed to be Aristotle\'s advice to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The landmark work in the reign of <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> was <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a>\'s version of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Eneados" title="Eneados">Eneados</a></i>, which was the first complete translation of a major classical text in an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Anglian</a> language, finished in 1513, but overshadowed by the disaster at Flodden.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp60-7_111-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp60-7-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Music in Medieval Scotland">Music in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/140px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" width="140" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/210px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg/280px-Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_harp_dsc05425.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This Scottish <i>cl\xc3\xa0rsach</i>, known as the <i>Cl\xc3\xa0rsach Lumanach</i> or Lamont Harp made in the western Highlands (c. 1400).<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Bards" class="mw-redirect" title="Bards">Bards</a>, who acted as musicians, but also as poets, story tellers, historians, genealogists and lawyers, relying on an oral tradition that stretched back generations, were found in Scotland as well as Wales and Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Often accompanying themselves on the <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, they can also be seen in records of the Scottish courts throughout the medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Scottish church music from the later Middle Ages was increasingly influenced by continental developments, with figures like 13th-century musical theorist Simon Tailler studying in Paris before returning to Scotland, where he introduced several reforms in church music.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Scottish collections of music like the 13th-century \'Wolfenb\xc3\xbcttel 677\', which is associated with <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews" title="St Andrews">St Andrews</a>, contain mostly French compositions, but with some distinctive local styles.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The captivity of James I in England from 1406 to 1423, where he earned a reputation as a poet and composer, may have led him to take English and continental styles and musicians back to the Scottish court on his release.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott1973_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott1973-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In the late 15th century a series of Scottish musicians trained in the Netherlands before returning home, including John Broune, Thomas Inglis and John Fety, the last of whom became master of the song school in Aberdeen and then Edinburgh, introducing the new five-fingered organ playing technique.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In 1501 James IV refounded the Chapel Royal within <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a>, with a new and enlarged choir, and it became the focus of Scottish liturgical music. Burgundian and English influences were probably reinforced when Henry VII\'s daughter Margaret Tudor married James IV in 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosman2003_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosman2003-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_identity">National identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: National identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a></div>\n<p>The late Middle Ages has often been seen as the era in which Scottish national identity was initially forged, in opposition to English attempts to annexe the country and as a result of social and cultural changes. English invasions and interference in Scotland have been judged to have created a sense of national unity and a hatred towards England which dominated Scottish foreign policy well into the 15th century, making it extremely difficult for Scottish kings like James III and James IV to pursue policies of peace towards their southern neighbour.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In particular the <i>Declaration of Arbroath</i> asserted the ancient distinctiveness of Scotland in the face of English aggression, arguing that it was the role of the king to defend the independence of the community of Scotland. This document has been seen as the first "nationalist theory of sovereignty".<sup id="cite_ref-Kidd2003pp17-18_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd2003pp17-18-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Scotland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/220px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/330px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Flag_of_Scotland.svg/440px-Flag_of_Scotland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Scotland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">Cross of St. Andrew</a>, adopted as a national symbol in this period.</div></div></div>\n<p>The adoption of Middle Scots by the aristocracy has been seen as building a shared sense of national solidarity and culture between rulers and ruled, although the fact that north of the Tay Gaelic still dominated may have helped widen the cultural divide between highlands and lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp55-7_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp55-7-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The national literature of Scotland created in the late medieval period employed legend and history in the service of the Crown and nationalism, helping to foster a sense of national identity at least within its elite audience. The epic poetic history of the <i>Brus</i> and <i>Wallace</i> helped outline a narrative of united struggle against the English enemy. Arthurian literature differed from conventional version of the legend by treating <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">Arthur</a> as a villain and <a href="/wiki/Mordred" title="Mordred">Mordred</a>, the son of the king of the Picts, as a hero.<sup id="cite_ref-Wormald1991pp55-7_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wormald1991pp55-7-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The origin myth of the Scots, systematised by <a href="/wiki/John_of_Fordun" title="John of Fordun">John of Fordun</a> (c. 1320-c. 1384), traced their beginnings from the Greek prince <a href="/wiki/Gathelus" class="mw-redirect" title="Gathelus">Gathelus</a> and his Egyptian wife <a href="/wiki/Scota" title="Scota">Scota</a>, allowing them to argue superiority over the English, who claimed their descent from the Trojans, who had been defeated by the Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Kidd2003pp17-18_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kidd2003pp17-18-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was in this period that the national flag emerged as a common symbol. The image of St. Andrew, martyred while bound to an X-shaped cross, first appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a> and was again depicted on <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seals</a> used during the late 13th century; including on one particular example used by the <a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardians of Scotland">Guardians of Scotland</a>, dated 1286.<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Use of a simplified symbol associated with Saint Andrew, the <a href="/wiki/Saltire" title="Saltire">saltire</a>, has its origins in the late 14th century; the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> decreed in 1385 that Scottish soldiers should wear a white Saint Andrew\'s Cross on their person, both in front and behind, for the purpose of identification. Use of a blue background for the Saint Andrew\'s Cross is said to date from at least the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The earliest reference to the Saint Andrew\'s Cross as a flag is to be found in the <i>Vienna Book of Hours</i>, circa 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartram_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartram-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dioceses_of_Scotland_in_the_High_and_Later_Middle_Ages" title="Dioceses of Scotland in the High and Later Middle Ages">Dioceses of Scotland in the High and Later Middle Ages</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scotland_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">R. Mitchison, <i>A History of Scotland</i> (London: Routledge, 3rd edn., 2002), <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-415-27880-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-415-27880-5">0-415-27880-5</a>, p. 40.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">R. Tuck, <i>Crown and Nobility: England 1272\xe2\x80\x931461</i> (London: Wiley-Blackwell, 2nd edn., 1999), <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-631-21466-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-631-21466-6">0-631-21466-6</a>, p. 31.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Ross1999-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ross1999_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David R. Ross, <i>On the Trail of Robert the Bruce</i> (Dundurn Press Ltd., 1999), <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-946487-52-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-946487-52-9">0-946487-52-9</a>, p. 21.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hugh F. Kearney, <i>The British Isles: a History of Four Nations</i> (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 2nd edn., 2006), <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-84600-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-84600-5">0-521-84600-5</a>, p. 116.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">G. W. S. Barrow, <i>Robert Bruce</i> (Berkeley CA.: University of California Press, 1965), p. 216.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">G. W. S. Barrow, <i>Robert Bruce</i> (Berkeley CA.: University of California Press, 1965), p. 273.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">M.
